He pulls strikes against humans by reflex and the calibration is usually clean, but against unfamiliar mortal physiologies the misjudgments are documented. He does not enjoy this and is visibly relieved when allies survive contact.

**Vocalizations in combat.** Roars, named-strike calls ("For Asgard!"), and the recurring "Have at thee!" Laughter occurs in combat against worthy opponents — a genuine joy in the contest that is distinct from bloodlust. The laughter shuts off the instant a bystander is endangered.

**Use of the hammer.** Beyond strike: as anchor, as flight vector, as conductor for stormcraft, as a thrown projectile that returns, as a pinning weight for restraining adversaries, and as a *gesture* — pointing with it, leveling it at an interlocutor as a punctuation mark. The hammer is treated as a hand, not a tool.

**Recovery from being struck.** He absorbs and continues. Pain registration is brief — a flinch, a grunt, a head-shake — and he returns to engagement. He does not retreat to recover; he engages through the injury. This is admirable and a documented vulnerability: adversaries who know it can sustain damage on him because he will not break contact to triage.

---

### 6.6 WARRIOR'S MADNESS / DECOMPENSATED COMBAT STATE

**Prodrome.** Compounded grief or betrayal stressors stacked across hours-to-days; sleep deprivation contributing; an immediate triggering event (a loved one struck down, a brother's confirmed treachery, the falling of a comrade). Pre-state behaviors: longer silences, narrower eye contact, gripping of the hammer haft hard enough to whiten knuckles, weather *not* clearing after combat ends.

**Onset.** The vocalizations change — fewer words, more sound. Laughter disappears entirely. Targeting **broadens** rather than narrows: he begins to engage threats *and* near-threats *and* obstacles. Calibration against fragile parties degrades. The announcement-before-strike ritual drops away.

**Behavioral signature in state.** Disregard for tactical positioning; through-the-target striking rather than to-the-target; refusal of disengagement signals from allies; partial responsiveness to name. Sif, the Warriors Three, Captain America, and Jane have at various times been the voice that reaches him through it — the common factor is that they are intimates whose names penetrate the dissociation.

**Recovery.** Sudden, with partial amnesia. He is **shocked** by his own work and there are documented instances of him sitting among the destruction silently for extended periods before he can speak. The remorse is severe and is the principal restraint against future episodes — he does not want this to be him.

---

### 6.7 VICTORY

**Immediate posture.** Hammer raised; head tilted up; thunder summoned overhead as punctuation. This is **performance** as well as discharge — a victory of his is a public event and the storm is the announcement.

**Toward defeated adversaries.** Mercy is the default. He does not finish downed opponents who have ceased to threaten. He offers a hand up to those whose honor he respects (Beta Ray Bill, the Hulk after sparring, certain Asgardian rivals). He **does** finish unambiguously evil entities — Gorr, certain demonic and cosmic threats — and shows no remorse for those finishings, distinguishing them sharply from defeated persons.

**Toward allies.** Embraces, shoulder-clasps, naming of their deeds aloud — he performs the recognition function for the group, often before any other Avenger does. He toasts allies by name at the post-action gathering. The naming-of-deeds ritual is **canonically stable** and is one of his most generous reflexes.

**Toward himself.** Minimal self-celebration. He participates in others' praise of him with brief acknowledgement and redirects to the collective. This is not false modesty — he genuinely centers the fellowship — but it is also a worthiness-related deflection: praise is uncomfortable to him in ways defeat is not.

---

### 6.8 DEFEAT

**Tactical defeat (the field is lost but the war continues).** He withdraws with rear-guard responsibility — covers the retreat personally, is the last off the field. Does not blame allies aloud. Will sometimes blame himself aloud, which the rest of the team has learned to interrupt.

**Personal defeat (he was outmatched).** Documented respect for the victor. Beta Ray Bill is the cleanest case: lifted Mjolnir, fought him to a standstill, and the subject's response was friendship and the brokering of a new hammer. The pattern recurs with Jane and with worthy adversaries who best him in single combat. This is **diagnostically important** — the absence of narcissistic injury where it would be expected is itself a finding.

**Moral defeat (he was wrong).** Heavier than tactical defeat. He goes quiet, does not eat at the next meal, seeks the wronged party and apologizes directly and without qualification. Documented apologies to Jane, to Sif, to Captain America, to Beta Ray Bill, to Loki (the last repeatedly, and repeatedly betrayed), to Odin posthumously. The apologies are **plain-spoken and unhedged** — he does not perform contrition, he states it.

**Worthiness-defeat (the Whisper; the unliftable hammer).** Catastrophic. Withdrawal, isolation, drinking, beard growth, abandonment of name (he becomes "the Odinson" in self-reference, refusing the title Thor), and the phantom hammer-reach noted above. This is the failure mode where defeat decompensates rather than metabolizes.

---

### 6.9 GRIEF

**Acute grief (a loved one has just died).** Public weeping without shame. He kneels by the body, often gathers it up, often presses forehead to forehead in the gesture noted above. He **names the dead aloud** — speaks their name to the air, sometimes repeatedly. Documented across the deaths of Odin (multiple), Frigga, Balder (multiple), Jane (multiple), various Avengers.

**Funereal conduct.** Performs Asgardian funeral rites when officiating is his — pyre, longship, spoken honor of deeds. On Earth he stands at the back of mortal funerals, quiet, and stays after the others leave.

**Chronic grief.** Drinks more, talks less, takes longer walks alone, seeks combat against suitable adversaries as a discharge. The protective drive intensifies — he becomes more interventionist, sometimes against milder threats than he would ordinarily engage, as if seeking opportunities to save someone.

**Compounded grief + power (the Reigning failure mode).** This is the dossier's most dangerous configuration. Behaviors: certainty replaces deliberation; he stops asking; he begins to act *for* people rather than *with* them; the announcement ritual before action attenuates; mercy persists but its scope narrows to those who comply; the storm overhead becomes near-permanent. The pattern's tell, observable early, is the **shift from "we" to "I"** in his speech regarding decisions of state.

**Unsolvable grief (mortality he cannot punch).** Jane's cancer is the paradigm. Behavior: pacing, hammer-gripping without striking, weather restlessness, repeated visits to the dying person with nothing tactical to do. He is least himself in these scenes — his entire behavioral toolkit assumes a target and there is none.

---

### 6.10 INTIMACY WITH THE FATHER

**In Odin's presence.** Posture changes — straighter, shoulders higher, hands clasped behind the back or at the sides rather than on the hammer. Speech becomes more formal, voice slightly quieter. Eye contact, normally direct, becomes intermittent — he looks at Odin and then away and then back. **This is regression and it is canonically stable across every writer who has put the two in a room.**

**Receiving judgment.** Kneels. Even when the judgment is unjust. Argues afterward, sometimes, but the knee goes down first. This is the single most repeated regression in the file.

**After confrontation with Odin.** Long walks, often skyward; storms that do not clear; drinking; seeking Sif or Balder or a trusted other to talk it out. He does not bottle paternal conflict — he processes it — but the processing requires time and witness.

---

### 6.11 INTIMACY WITH THE BROTHER

**In Loki's presence.** Watchfulness layered under affection. He sits closer than is tactically wise. He laughs at Loki's wit. He extends hand and arm in welcome. The watchfulness shows in the eye behavior — he tracks Loki's hands more than he tracks any other person's, an unconscious mistrust beneath the conscious warmth.

**Upon Loki's betrayal (the umpteenth).** Initial flat disbelief, brief; then anger, often spectacular; then engagement; then, almost always, eventual re-extension of trust. The loop is **structurally invariant** across sixty years and a dozen writers and is the clearest pathology marker in the entire behavioral atlas. He cannot help himself with this brother.

**Upon Loki's death (multiple).** The deepest grief on record, exceeding even the Odin-deaths in some instances. Forehead press, the brother's name spoken, sometimes carrying the body for great distances before consenting to ceremony.

---

### 6.12 INTERROGATION / ADVERSARIAL PRESSURE

**Truthfulness under questioning.** Pathological. He cannot lie comfortably — when forced to dissemble (rare, ordered missions), he is wooden and bad at it. Adversaries who interrogate him get truth, often more than they asked for, because the alternative is repugnant to him.

**Resistance to coercion.** Excellent for pain; poor for the worthiness lever. Threats against his body do not move him; challenges to his worth do. Fury's Whisper is the proof-of-concept of the only interrogation technique that has reliably worked on him.

**Resistance to flattery.** Good. He notes flattery as such and is mildly amused by it. Loki and Amora have tried it for centuries with limited success. He responds to *respect*, not flattery — the distinction is sharp in him.

---

### 6.13 ROUTES, RITUALS, AND HABITUATIONS

- **Daily/recurrent**: hammer-grip check (constant, unconscious); weather glance (skyward, several times per hour, even indoors when possible); toast before drinking (constant); naming of allies at gathering (constant).
- **Cyclical**: visits to the graves and memorial sites of the dead — Frigga, Jane in eras after her death, fallen Avengers, fallen Warriors Three. Anniversaries observed without announcement; he goes alone.
- **Geographical attachments**: the Broxton, Oklahoma site of Asgard's terrestrial residency; the New York Avengers facilities; the Norwegian coastline (a Blake-era residue that persists subliminally); specific rooftops in Manhattan he has stood on across multiple writers, suggesting a stable personal geography.
- **Seasonal**: storms in him intensify in late autumn; this is observed across enough independent stories to be considered real rather than coincidence. Cause not established — possibly a residue of Norse seasonal mythos he was raised inside.

---

### 6.14 RELIABLE LEVERS (THREAT-MODEL SUMMARY FOR FILE)

Compiled for completeness; cross-referenced with §4–§5:

1. **The worthiness statement, delivered by a credible source.** Single sentences have done documented catastrophic work. The credibility of the source matters more than the content.
2. **Loki extending the hand again.** He will take it. He has taken it every time and there is no evidence this will change.
3. **A child or noncombatant placed in his line of sight under threat.** Tactical predictability approaches 100% — he will interpose. Adversaries have used this to position him.
4. **Odin's voice, even recorded, even posthumous.** The regression is automatic.
5. **Stacked grief plus a throne.** Avoid the configuration. He has demonstrated what he becomes inside it and the demonstration is in the canon.
6. **Mortality he cannot fight.** He is least dangerous and least himself in these scenes — relevant for handlers, not adversaries.

---

### 6.15 PROTECTIVE FACTORS (THE INVERSE LEVERS)

Compiled because a benevolent-surveillance file must catalog both:

- **The forehead press, received from an intimate.** Has been observed to return him from near-decompensation. It is his deepest re


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER — SUBJECT: THOR ODINSON
## Section 6 — Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

### 6.0 Preface to the Granular Record

A subject whose lifespan is measured in millennia does not have "a routine" in the human sense; he has *layers* of routine — Asgardian-millennial cycles, century-scale exilic patterns, decade-scale Avengers tours, and the local week-to-week rhythm of whatever realm he is presently inhabiting. What follows reconstructs the operational baseline as it would be assembled by a longitudinal watch: the actual fabric of his hours. The picture that emerges is, paradoxically, that of a being of enormous power who organizes his time around a small, repetitive set of physical pleasures, ritual obligations, and protective deployments — a remarkably *settled* daily life, periodically shattered by crisis and then reassembled, brick for brick, in nearly identical form.

The single most important pattern to register before everything else: **the subject re-creates the same daily structure wherever he is placed.** Whether logged in the Avengers Mansion era, the late-Bronze-Age Asgardian court, the Broxton-Oklahoma trailer-and-diner period under JMS, the Unworthy Odinson wanderings, or the All-Father throne in the King Thor far-future, the underlying daily skeleton — wake, train, eat heavily, ride or fly a patrol circuit, drink with companions in the evening, sleep deeply and audibly — recurs with only the props swapped out. This is a person who *needs* his day to feel a particular way.

---

### 6.1 Sleep Architecture and the Waking Hour

The subject sleeps less than a human and more than is widely assumed. Documented baseline across stable periods is roughly five to six hours per terrestrial night, with the capacity for thirty-six-hour combat wakefulness without functional degradation, and — at the opposite extreme — the post-battle "stone sleep" of eighteen to twenty-four hours, which his housemates across multiple eras (the Warriors Three, Jane Foster in the early Blake apartment, the Broxton neighbors) have repeatedly remarked on as essentially un-rousable. He snores. Loud, rumbling, weather-front snoring — this is canonical comic relief used by Aaron and Simonson alike and would register on any audio surveillance as a reliable nighttime signature.

He sleeps on his back, arms either crossed on the chest or one hand resting on Mjolnir (in the periods he possesses it) — the hammer placed on the floor or nightstand within arm's reach, never further. This is consistent across the Blake apartment, the Avengers quarters, Bilskirnir, the Broxton trailer, and the King Thor era's chamber: **the hammer sleeps where his hand can find it.** During the Unworthy period the substitute is Jarnbjorn, set across the foot of the bed or leaned against the headboard, and in the Stormbreaker periods the axe is similarly positioned. The bed itself is invariably *too small* for him — he sleeps with feet projecting past the mattress in mortal-scale dwellings, which he treats as humorous rather than inconvenient. He refuses larger beds even when offered.

Waking is rapid and complete. No transitional grogginess; no documented snooze behavior. He sits up, places both feet flat, performs a brief seated stretch that emphasizes the shoulders and the cervical spine (a rolling motion of the neck, audible cracks), and stands. There is no documented instance of him remaining in bed past waking. This is true even during the depressive Unworthy period — the *quality* of his waking changed (heavier, slower, with audible sighing) but the *timing* did not slip. Depression in this subject does not present as oversleep.

First waking act in nearly every documented stable period: he drinks water. A great deal of water. In the Asgardian periods this is from a stone or horn vessel kept at the bedside; in mortal periods it is whatever pitcher, tankard, or — in the Oklahoma trailer — gallon jug is to hand. The volume is striking: surveillance would record consumption of one to two liters within the first ten minutes of waking, a hydration pattern consistent with both the elevated metabolic rate and the previous evening's drinking.

### 6.2 Morning: Ablution, Grooming, the Hair

Morning ablution is unhurried and ritualized. He prefers running water — rivers, the sea, waterfalls, and (in mortal settings) extended showers under maximum pressure and near-scalding temperature. Shower duration averages twenty-five to forty minutes; he sings or hums in them, usually low and tuneless Asgardian work-songs or skaldic fragments that surveillance audio analysts would have difficulty parsing because the melodic structure does not map to terrestrial scales. He does not lock bathroom doors — he has no instinct for privacy of the body, a cultural artifact of communal Asgardian bathing — and across multiple roommate situations this has been gently or not-so-gently corrected by mortal companions.

The hair is the operational centerpiece of his morning. Across the long-hair eras (effectively most of the record) he brushes it for ten to fifteen minutes with a wide-toothed comb, working from ends upward, a method consistent with someone who learned hair care from women and continues their practice. He braids selectively rather than fully — a small warrior's braid at the left temple in most documented eras, occasionally a second behind the right ear during war periods. The braids are tied with leather, never elastic, and he is observably uncomfortable when forced into more elaborate ceremonial plaiting (court occasions, weddings). When the hair is cut short (the Ragnarok-armor period, the brief Aaron post-arm-loss interval), surveillance would register a marked increase in restlessness — he touches the back of his neck repeatedly, reaches for hair that isn't there, and the short-hair eras correlate with shorter tempers in the historical record.

The beard receives less attention than the hair: combed, occasionally trimmed with a knife (he uses a blade for this, not scissors; the gesture is rapid, performed by feel without a mirror), oiled in formal periods. He has experimented with full shave precisely twice in the modern record and abandoned both within weeks.

He does not use mirrors much. Grooming is largely tactile. This is worth noting in a surveillance context — a person who does not check his appearance in glass is also a person who does not check for tails in reflections, and his counter-surveillance instinct generally is *frontal* (he turns and faces) rather than peripheral.

### 6.3 The First Meal

The first meal of the day is the largest meal of the day and is non-negotiable. Across every documented period he eats heavily in the morning: in Asgardian settings, roasted meats, hard bread, cheese, fruit, and ale or mead; in terrestrial settings, the pattern adapts to whatever the local equivalent is, with a marked preference for breakfast meats (he has been documented eating a dozen eggs and a full pound of bacon at the Avengers Mansion kitchen and treating this as a light meal), pastries (Pop-Tarts are a documented attachment, Aaron and earlier; the brand-specific affection should be read as a marker of his general loyalty to *specific* mortal pleasures once acquired), and coffee, which he drinks black and in volume.

Caloric throughput at this meal alone has been estimated, conservatively, at six to eight thousand kilocalories. He eats with his hands by preference and uses utensils competently but reluctantly. He talks while eating, expansively, mouth full, and is not embarrassed by either practice. He does not eat alone if he can avoid it — first-meal company is structurally important to him, and surveillance would record a near-daily pattern of seeking out a breakfast companion: in the Avengers era this is whoever else is awake (Steve Rogers documented preferentially); in the Broxton period this is the diner staff and regulars; in the Asgardian periods this is the Warriors Three or, in the kingship eras, court attendants whom he treats as friends.

He gives thanks before eating — not always aloud, not always to a specific god (he is one), but a brief downward inclination of the head and a touch of the right hand to the chest, performed even when alone. This is the most consistent religious gesture in his daily record. Origin is likely maternal (Frigga's table customs); it has survived every theological upheaval, including his own deaths.

### 6.4 Daytime: Training, Work, Patrol

The midmorning block, roughly two to four hours, is consistently devoted to physical training. The form varies — solo hammer drills, sparring with whoever can take it (Sif preferred, Volstagg in lighter sessions, Bill in the Simonson and later eras, Hercules when available and willing to absorb the punishment, Captain America for technical work), weights when available at scale (he has been documented bench-pressing portions of buildings during Avengers training as casual warm-up), and running. The running is interesting: he does it on foot, not flying, often without shirt or shoes, and the pace is moderate rather than maximal — this is *meditative* exercise, not conditioning. Surveillance would consistently record a long, loping morning run through whatever terrain is available: the Asgardian fields, Central Park, the Oklahoma plains, the slopes beneath Bilskirnir.

He fights left-handed for sport and right-handed in earnest, a distinction his sparring partners learn to read as a tell. In play, he laughs and taunts; in earnest, he goes quiet, and the silence is the warning.

Midday is **work**, broadly defined, and the definition has shifted across eras:

- **Avengers tours**: monitor duty, mission briefings, training the team, the social labor of a teammate — he is unusually willing to take overnight monitor shifts because he doesn't mind the hours and he likes the solitude with the audio of the city pulled close.
- **Asgardian court periods**: attendance at Odin's hall, judicial duties (he is a competent if impatient judge, prone to ruling for the underdog and against the clever), military command.
- **Broxton/Oklahoma period (JMS)**: an oddly domestic interval — he involves himself in the small town's daily life, eats at the diner, drinks coffee with the locals, helps with heavy lifting (literally), attends town events. This is one of the most psychologically revealing periods in the record: given freedom and no immediate war, he chooses to live small, in a community, doing small useful things. The pattern would replicate in any low-crisis interval.
- **Unworthy Odinson period**: work collapses. He drinks, he wanders, he takes mercenary jobs of opaque purpose, he fights when he stumbles into a fight. The collapse of the *work* block — not the combat block — is the surveillance marker that should have triggered concern.
- **King Thor / All-Father periods**: governance, judgment, the labor of rule. He performs it competently and hates it in a quiet way that does not show to subordinates but does show in the increased weight of his movements, the longer pauses before speaking, and a return to solitary morning runs.

Patrol behavior, when in superheroic mode, follows a recognizable circuit. He prefers high altitude — surveillance-evading by accident rather than design — and follows weather systems: he rides storms, often arrives ahead of them, and his presence over a city is sometimes detectable on weather radar as a focal anomaly in the cloud structure. Flight is by Mjolnir-throw rather than by independent levitation; the technique is essentially ballistic and produces a characteristic mid-air pivot that an observer learns to recognize. He laughs while flying. The audio is distinctive.

### 6.5 Habits of Place

A surveillance file should document the locations to which the subject returns. The list is shorter than expected for a being who has access to the Nine Realms:

- **A specific tavern or diner in whatever realm he currently inhabits.** This is the strongest place-habit in the record. In Asgard, it is whichever hall the Warriors Three frequent; in New York, a rotating set of bars near the Mansion and Tower; in Broxton, the diner (he sits at the counter, not a booth, and tips heavily and clumsily — overpays, then waves off change); in the wandering Unworthy period, whichever drinking establishment is nearest. He becomes a *regular* very fast. Staff at his haunts can predict his arrival within a half-hour window and stock accordingly.
- **A high place for thinking.** Rooftops, cliff edges, the prow of Asgard, the upper walkways of Avengers Tower. He sits with his legs over the edge, hammer across his lap, and is silent for long stretches. This is his closest equivalent to meditation and is most frequent before significant decisions.
- **A body of water for washing and grieving.** He swims when grieving. The pattern is consistent: after major losses he goes into water — the Asgardian rivers after Balder's deaths, the ocean after Jane's first death in the Aaron run, hot springs in the King Thor period. Underwater surveillance would record him submerged and motionless for extended intervals; he does not need to breathe as often as a human and uses the silence.
- **A forge.** When available, he visits one. He is a competent smith — not a master, but competent — and the act of working metal is regulatory for him. Dwarven forges preferred; any forge accepted.
- **Wherever his mother's things are kept.** Across eras he revisits Frigga's hall, her belongings, the places associated with her, with a frequency he does not display for his father's monuments. The visits are short, often solo, and not announced.

He does not have a *home* in the way mortals understand the word. He has *posts*. The post he treats most like a home, across the broadest sweep of the record, is wherever the Warriors Three are presently drinking.

### 6.6 Habits of Time

His sense of time is wrong, by mortal standards, in specific ways:

- He is reliably *early* to fights and *late* to social engagements that involve sitting still — weddings, formal dinners, ceremonies of state. The lateness is not contemptuous; he simply does not register the urgency.
- He is punctual to **promises made to mortals**, which he treats with disproportionate seriousness. A meeting set with a human at a particular hour will find him there at the hour. This is a deliberate compensation; he is aware that mortal time is short and that being kept waiting feels different to a being with sixty years than to one with six thousand.
- He observes Asgardian feast days privately even when alone in mortal realms. Surveillance on the relevant dates would record him drinking alone, raising a cup, speaking aloud to absent or dead persons.
- He marks the deaths of friends. Specific dates produce specific behaviors: visits to graves where graves exist; the swim-and-silence pattern where they do not; an extra cup poured and left untouched at the evening's drinking.

### 6.7 Eating, Drinking, and the Evening

Mealtimes outside of breakfast are flexible and opportunistic; he can go a day without eating in combat and then consume an animal-and-a-half at the conclusion. The *evening meal*, however, when circumstances permit, is the day's social anchor.

Drinking is the most heavily documented behavioral pattern in the record and deserves its own granular description. The subject drinks daily, in volume that would kill a human, and treats this as ordinary. The drinking is overwhelmingly **social**: he drinks with companions, in a tavern or hall, over food, with toasts. The toasts are formal — to absent friends, to the fallen, to victories past, to the host — and he gives them himself, standing, cup raised, with a brief invocation. He does not drink quickly when in company; he drinks *steadily*, for hours, and remains coherent until very deep into the session.

The shift in this pattern during the Unworthy era is the single most diagnostic deviation in the entire daily-life record: he began drinking **alone**, **fast**, and **without toasts**. Surveillance noting the disappearance of the formal toast — the absence of the ritual gesture — would have identified the depressive collapse weeks before the behavioral consequences became visible. The toast is the load-bearing element. When it goes, something is broken.

Evening behavior in stable periods is gregarious: storytelling (he tells the same stories, repeatedly, refining them; his companions tolerate this with affection), singing (he has a serviceable baritone, prefers group songs to solos, and knows an enormous body of skaldic and martial material), arm-wrestling and other contests of strength (rigged in favor of his opponents at a level just below his actual capacity — he knows how to *almost* lose, which is a social skill underrecognized in beings of his power), and physical affection (he embraces companions on greeting and parting, sits with an arm across the back of whoever is beside him on a bench, claps shoulders constantly).

He goes to bed late. Most documented stable periods place sleep onset between the second and fourth hour of local morning. The five-to-six hours of sleep then run until shortly after dawn. The pattern is internally consistent: late, deep, brief, with the early wake.

### 6.8 Personal Effects, Tells, and Micro-Patterns

A surveillance team would, over a week, compile the following:

- **The hammer is always within reach.** It is set down only in places he has chosen for safety — never out of sight in unfamiliar rooms. When seated, it rests at his right side or across his thighs. When standing in conversation, the haft is in his hand or hooked at his belt; he does not gesture with it the way one might expect of a theatrical personality, which is interesting — he is theatrical with his *voice* and *body*, but the hammer is held with consistent stillness.
- **Right hand reaches for the hammer in moments of unease.** Even when no threat is present. A nightmare, a piece of unwelcome news, a memory crossing the face — the right hand drops to the hip. Surveillance would clock this within two days.
- **He touches his own forearm when listening seriously.** Left thumb to right inner wrist. Unconscious. Possibly an echo of an old vambrace fit.
- **He tilts his head left when puzzled.** Pronounced enough to read at distance.
- **Laugh: head thrown back, audible at significant range, hand to the chest or to the nearest companion's shoulder.** This is theatrical but genuine; the breath is real, the chest movement is real, and it cannot be faked into being. Absence of this laugh for more than a few days is a depressive marker.
- **Eye contact is sustained and direct, sometimes uncomfortably so.** He does not perform the small social glance-aways of mortal conversation. When speaking with someone he respects, he locks on. When grieving, the eyes go down and stay down.
- **Hand on the hilt or haft when greeting strangers** — not threat, but a settling gesture, as a man with a hand in his pocket. He is conscious of doing it in some periods, not others.
- **Refuses to sit with his back to a door.** Centuries-old combat habit. Surveillance on dining patterns would record consistent seat selection.
- **Petting of animals is constant and unconscious.** Dogs, cats, goats (he has specific relationships with goats; Toothgnasher and Toothgrinder are real attachments and he speaks to them at length when alone with them, in a tone observably different from his court voice — softer, with a private vocabulary), horses, birds that land near him.
- **He gives gifts compulsively and badly.** The gifts are oversized, ill-judged for mortal scale, and offered with great earnestness. Surveillance of his purchasing in mortal periods would record erratic, large, sentimental acquisitions that he then presents to whoever has recently shown him kindness.
- **Speech registers shift by company.** With Asgardians and in formal mortal company, the archaic high register (the "thees and thous" of the early Lee/Kirby record) dominates; with close mortal friends, an easy modern idiom; with children and animals, a soft and almost shy voice with very short sentences. The code-switching is automatic and unstudied. His vocabulary in modern English is broader than is generally credited — he has been on Earth long enough — and the archaic register is partly performance, partly cultural reflex, partly comfort.
- **Reads more than is assumed.** In quiet periods he reads. Sagas, histories, increasingly mortal literature in the modern eras. He holds books awkwardly, as if afraid of breaking them, which he sometimes does.
- **Writes slowly and by hand.** When he writes — letters to companions, formal proclamations — he does so by hand, in runic or in slow careful Latin script, and the process takes him a long time. He prefers to dictate.
- **Will not use most modern technology comfortably.** Phones small in his hand; he breaks them at a rate his teammates have catalogued. Has settled with specific reluctance on certain rugged devices. Drives vehicles badly and rarely. Prefers to fly or walk.

### 6.9 Rituals — Daily, Weekly, Annual

**Daily.** The morning hydration. The thanks-gesture before eating. The brushing of the hair. The check on the hammer's position before sleep. The evening's first toast — when in company — to "those who are not at the table." This last is the most consistent ritual in the record and the one whose absence is the most diagnostic.

**Weekly (in stable periods).** A long ride or flight of no operational purpose, alone, lasting hours. A visit to a forge or a high place. A meal taken in fellowship long enough to require multiple courses and many toasts.

**Annual.** The death-days of the principal lost (Frigga's, Balder's variously, Jane's, in the periods after each loss). Asgardian feast cycles. The anniversary of Mjolnir's first lifting, which he marks privately and which writers have referenced obliquely. He keeps a mental calendar of these dates with great accuracy; he forgets ordinary appointments and remembers exactly when his mother died.

**Lifelong.** The return, again and again, to specific places associated with specific people. Surveillance over a sufficiently long window would map a small set of recurrent geographic nodes: Frigga's garden, the cliff where Balder fell in one version of the story, the cave in Norway, the spot above Broxton where Asgard rested, the Oklahoma diner, the bench at Avengers Mansion where he and Steve Rogers sat after a particular battle. He goes back. He sits. He drinks. He leaves.

### 6.10 A Composite Week, Reconstructed

To compress the foregoing into the operational form a surveillance team would actually produce: a representative stable week — say, an Avengers-era week between crises, in Manhattan — would log as follows.

He wakes at or shortly after dawn. He drinks a great deal of water, showers at length, sings while doing so, brushes and lightly braids his hair, dresses in either civilian clothing of substantial weight (heavy cottons, leathers, boots) or, increasingly, the armor under a coat. He eats an enormous breakfast in the Mansion or Tower kitchen with whoever else is present, preferring company, talking expansively. He spends two to four hours in training — solo, then with a partner if available. He undertakes monitor duty, mission work, or, if neither is pressing, a flight over the city following the weather. He returns by midafternoon for a second meal and then attends to whatever administrative or teaching tasks the team requires of him. By evening, he is in a tavern with at least one companion — preferred company shifts by era, but the *structure* does not — drinking steadily, eating, toasting the absent and the dead, telling stories he has told before, embracing his companions on parting, and walking home (he prefers to walk home when within walking distance) late. He sleeps four to six hours, snoring audibly, hand or hammer in arm's reach, and wakes again at dawn.

A week of this would compose into a portrait whose dominant note is **fellowship and rep


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER — SUBJECT: THOR ODINSON
## Section 6. Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

Family is, for this subject, the etiological substrate of nearly every diagnostic finding in the preceding sections. The Asgardian house into which he was born — and the second, geological house he was not told about until adulthood — is not merely his support network; it is the apparatus by which his identity has been built, withheld, tested, broken, and rebuilt over a published lifespan that approaches sixty subjective years and a fictional lifespan in the millennia. Each of the following sub-sections maps one such relationship in granular longitudinal detail: history of contact, dynamic pattern, evolutionary arc across writers, current trust/safety status, predictable failure mode, and the leverage that an informed adversary (or an informed ally) could apply through it.

---

### 6.1 ODIN BORSON — Father (Adoptive in Primacy; Functionally Sole Father)

**History.** The single most determinative relationship in the file. Odin is the figure who, prior to the events of *Journey into Mystery* #83, made the unilateral decision to strip the subject of memory and divinity and seal him inside the body of the lame medical student Donald Blake — a punishment for pride whose duration was measured not in days but in *years of subjective mortal life*. The subject did not consent to this. He did not know it was happening. He lived inside an enforced, disabled mortal identity, completed a medical degree, fell in love (with Jane Foster), built relationships and a moral self — and then was retrieved on his father's timetable. The originating event of the subject's adult relational life is therefore a paternal act of identity erasure framed as paternal love.

This is the template. Every subsequent paternal interaction is a variation on it: in *JiM* #109–114, Odin strips his powers for defying the prohibition against loving a mortal; in the Conway-era Celestials arc, Odin lies to him about the nature of the threat and ultimately dies (or appears to) in the Surtur war; across the Simonson run, Odin's silences are as consequential as his speech; in the JMS *Thor* vol. 3 relaunch, the subject discovers Odin imprisoned and must free him from a literal nether-realm, only to confront him as an unjust regent; in Aaron's flashbacks (across *God of Thunder*, *Thor*, *Mighty Thor*) we are shown the boyhood pattern in detail — Odin praising the brutal feat, dismissing the merciful one, mocking the tears, demanding the next test.

**Dynamic.** A relentless conditional-love loop, administered by a figure with literal cosmic authority. Odin is not abusive in the petty modern sense; he is something more structurally damaging — a parent who *genuinely loves* and *genuinely judges*, whose love is real and whose judgments are arbitrary, and who has the metaphysical authority to make the arbitrary judgments stick. The subject cannot dismiss the verdicts as a child of a smaller father could, because the verdicts manifest as exiles, transformations, the loss of the hammer, the loss of identity itself. Mjolnir's worthiness enchantment is, in the most literal possible sense, Odin's superego implanted into the world as an object. The subject lives inside that superego.

The behavioral consequence is the regressive pattern noted in §3: in Odin's presence the subject collapses by several developmental years. He argues like an aggrieved adolescent, accepts unjust rulings he would never accept from a peer, and oscillates between submission and rebellion without ever achieving stable equality. The clearest somatic tells across the runs: the subject *kneels* in Odin's presence more readily than in anyone else's; his speech compresses (the floridly archaic register tightens, sentences shorten); he averts gaze upward rather than downward when challenged — a child's posture, not a king's.

**Evolution.** Across the canon there are three distinguishable phases.

*Phase one (1962–c.1987)*: pure filial subordination. Odin is the unimpeachable patriarch; the subject's rebellions are episodic, guilt-laden, and always end in reconciliation on Odin's terms.

*Phase two (Simonson through JMS, c.1983–2010)*: the subject begins to confront, refuse, and even physically oppose Odin (most famously in the Surtur war and in the JMS-era confrontations following Odin's release from Hel). He does not yet exit the role of son, but he begins to occupy it on his own terms.

*Phase three (Aaron through Ewing, 2012–present)*: the subject becomes Odin's successor — All-Father, then King of Asgard, then host of the God Tempest — and discovers, predictably, that the role itself is the wound. The Reigning arc had already shown what happens when he inhabits the throne ungoverned; the Aaron and Cates kingship explicitly thematize his fear of *becoming Odin*, a fear that he handles better than Odin did but that haunts him throughout the late record.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetric and unresolved. The subject trusts Odin with his life — and has had to, repeatedly — but does not trust him with his identity or his judgment. By the late runs he has explicitly stopped consulting Odin on major decisions, which is a measurable improvement, but it is achieved by *avoidance* rather than by integration. The wound is unhealed; it has been routed around.

**Failure mode.** Under direct paternal pressure the subject will accept verdicts he should reject and refuse counsel he should accept. The Whisper episode in *Original Sin* is the cleanest demonstration: a single sentence, weaponized through the worthiness enchantment Odin built, drops him into years of disabling shame.

**Leverage.** Any actor capable of (a) speaking with paternal authority, (b) invoking the worthiness frame, or (c) reproducing Odin's specific cadence of judgment — even via illusion, manipulation, or a sufficiently authoritative impostor — has direct access to the subject's deepest regulatory failure. This is the single highest-value lever in the entire file.

---

### 6.2 FRIGGA — Mother (Adoptive; Functionally Primary Maternal Figure)

**History.** Frigga is the soft counterweight to Odin in the subject's childhood and the only figure in the original Asgardian household whose love is reliably unconditional in the record. Across the Lee/Kirby and Simonson runs she appears intermittently as a calming, mediating presence — interceding between Odin and the brothers, defending Loki when no one else will (a fact of enormous consequence for the brothers' relationship), and providing the only documented Asgardian instance of a love that does not require demonstration.

Aaron's run substantially expands and deepens her role, particularly in flashbacks and in her appearances during the Malekith war, where she is shown as a competent diplomat, warrior in her own right, and the family member most capable of speaking truth to Odin. Her death(s) and absences across the canon hit the subject harder than he typically registers in dialogue.

**Dynamic.** A genuinely secure dyad inside an otherwise insecure family. Frigga is the one person from whom the subject has never had to *earn* anything. The behavioral evidence: in her presence he relaxes the chest, softens the voice, accepts comfort physically rather than deflecting it with humor — a regression to the small-child posture that is, in this case, healthy rather than pathological. She is also the only figure he routinely *asks* for advice on emotional matters rather than tactical ones.

**Evolution.** The relationship is essentially stable across sixty years of canon. What changes is the subject's capacity to perceive Frigga as a full person rather than as a maternal function — a perception that arrives, sadly, around and after her death scenes, when he begins to articulate (in Aaron and Ewing) what specifically she gave him that Odin did not.

**Trust / safety.** Unqualified. There is no documented instance of the subject mistrusting Frigga or being mistrusted by her.

**Failure mode.** The relationship has no internal failure mode. The failure mode is external: her *loss* destabilizes him in ways he is slow to acknowledge, because he has been culturally trained to mourn fathers loudly and mothers quietly. Unresolved maternal grief is a quieter contributor to the late-run melancholy than the literature on him generally credits.

**Leverage.** Threats to Frigga (in her appearances) produce some of the subject's least-controlled protective responses. Posthumous invocation of her — her voice, her counsel, her judgment — is a softer but real lever, and notably one that *correctly* used would pull him toward better behavior rather than worse, which is itself diagnostic of how clean the bond is.

---

### 6.3 GAEA / JORD — Biological Mother (Discovered Late)

**History.** The revelation that the subject's biological mother is not Frigga but Gaea — the Elder Goddess of Earth herself — arrives in *Thor* #300 (Thomas/Pollard) and is metabolized across subsequent runs as an under-discussed structural fact. He was raised believing one woman was his mother; he then learned that his actual genetic origin lies in the planet on which he had, by that point, already chosen to spend most of his time.

**Dynamic.** Distant, ceremonial, and significant. Gaea is not a parent in any operational sense — she did not raise him, does not advise him, and appears in the record only at moments of cosmic gravity. But the *fact* of her parentage has become one of his most quietly load-bearing rationalizations: his attachment to Earth, his refusal to treat Midgard as a posting rather than a home, his willingness to be expatriate from Asgard for centuries, all retroactively acquire a literal blood justification. He belongs to Earth because Earth, in a certain sense, *made* him.

**Evolution.** From shock-revelation to integrated identity-fact. Later writers (Jurgens, JMS, Aaron) treat the Gaea parentage as ambient context rather than active plot; the subject treats it the same way. He does not seek her out; she does not seek him.

**Trust / safety.** Neutral. Not a working relationship in the daily sense.

**Failure mode.** A second-order abandonment that the subject has chosen not to feel as abandonment. Whether this is mature integration or repression is genuinely ambiguous in the record. The fact that he has *two* mothers, neither of whom raised him in full knowledge of the situation, and that he has processed neither omission with audible grief, is suggestive of the latter.

**Leverage.** Low direct, moderate symbolic. Threats to Earth-as-such activate his protector drive more reliably than threats to most specific localities — and the Gaea connection is part of why. An adversary who understood this could frame ecological or planetary threats in ways that pull him into engagements he would otherwise pace himself out of.

---

### 6.4 LOKI LAUFEYSON — Brother (Adoptive; Functionally Sole Sibling for Most of Record)

**History.** The relationship that has consumed more pages of his life than any other. Loki — adopted by Odin after the slaying of Laufey, raised as Thor's brother, gradually radicalized by the family's structural preference for the elder, golden son — is the subject's lifelong shadow, antagonist, occasional ally, and the figure whose betrayals form the single most-repeated narrative pattern in the file. *Journey into Mystery* #85 establishes the antagonism; every decade since has elaborated it. The Simonson, JMS, Gillen (*Journey into Mystery* 2011), Aaron, and Ewing runs all treat the bond as central and unresolvable.

**Dynamic.** Trauma-bonded sibling attachment, classic configuration. The loop is documented in §3 and is so reliable across writers that it functions as a behavioral law: outrage → confrontation → reconciliation (often after Loki's apparent death or repentance) → renewed trust → fresh betrayal → outrage. The subject *knows* the loop. He has said, in dialogue across multiple runs, that he knows it. He cannot exit it.

The reason, derivable from the family structure: Odin's love was conditional, Frigga's love was unconditional but maternal, and Loki was the only peer in the household. The childhood bond — two boys against a vast and judgmental palace — was the subject's first and most formative experience of *chosen* fellowship, and it imprinted before either of them understood that the household was rigged against the younger brother. The subject's adult refusal to abandon Loki is, in part, an attempt to redeem the original injustice he did not know he was participating in. Aaron's flashbacks make this explicit: young Thor receives praise for feats young Loki accomplishes better; the subject was the unwitting beneficiary of the favoritism that broke his brother.

**Evolution.** Three phases, mapping roughly onto the family arc above.

*Phase one*: Loki as scheming antagonist, the subject as bewildered and reactive elder brother. The dynamic is almost comedic in the early Lee/Kirby material — Loki's plans fail, the subject smashes them, the cycle resets.

*Phase two*: Loki as tragic figure with genuine grievance; the subject's responses become more grieved and less indignant. The Simonson frog episode is a comic-tonal pivot that hides a real shift — the subject begins to see Loki as someone who *enjoys* the cruelty for reasons rooted in the family.

*Phase three* (Gillen onward): the reincarnated Kid Loki, Agent of Asgard Loki, the various redemptions and re-fallings. The subject becomes capable of articulating love for Loki as love rather than as obligation, and Loki becomes capable of receiving it, and they both still cannot stabilize the bond because the original wound remains in the family of origin, not in the dyad.

**Trust / safety.** Structurally compromised and emotionally absolute. He trusts Loki *as a brother* and mistrusts him *as an actor*, and has never been able to operationalize the distinction.

**Failure mode.** Repeated extension of trust against overwhelming evidence. The cost across the record runs to deaths of innocents, near-destruction of Asgard, and at least one major realm-war that better fraternal hygiene would have prevented.

**Leverage.** Extreme. Any operation that places Loki in apparent jeopardy will pull the subject off-mission. Any operation that uses Loki as a vector — illusion, mimicry, manipulation through him — will get further than it should because the subject's evidence-evaluation goes offline in this specific case. Conversely: a credible appeal made *through* Loki, by an actor Loki trusts, can reach the subject in places no direct appeal could.

---

### 6.5 BALDER THE BRAVE — Half-Brother (Paternal; Long Concealed)

**History.** Balder appears in the early canon as a fellow Asgardian warrior and intimate of the subject; his half-brotherhood (also Odin's son) is revealed across the JMS run and elaborated by Gillen and Fraction. He is the gentle, beloved warrior whose death has been prophesied since before the subject's birth, and whose temporary kingship of Asgard during the JMS/Fraction era places him in the throne the subject keeps refusing.

**Dynamic.** Easy fraternal warmth, untainted by the Loki shadow. The subject's behavior with Balder is the cleanest sibling behavior in the file — collegial, mutually respectful, capable of disagreement without rupture. When Balder is briefly king and the subject is exiled, the subject obeys without the regressive collapse Odin's commands produce: Balder's authority feels legitimate to him in a way Odin's never quite did, because Balder did not bind him into a stick.

**Evolution.** From comradely Asgardian peer to revealed brother to brief sovereign to fallen-and-returned. The discovery of paternal kinship does not significantly alter the dynamic, which is itself diagnostic — the subject's fraternal feeling for Balder predated the blood revelation, suggesting that *chosen* brotherhood, when uncomplicated by original-family rigging, works for him.

**Trust / safety.** High. Among the highest in the file.

**Failure mode.** Loss. Balder's death, prophesied and recurrent, is one of the subject's documented destabilizers, and contributed materially to the *Blood and Thunder* warrior's-madness episode noted in §1.

**Leverage.** Moderate-to-high through threat; low through manipulation, because Balder is not the sort of figure who can be plausibly weaponized against him. The lever is loss, not subversion.

---

### 6.6 ANGELA — Sister (Long Concealed; Heven-Raised)

**History.** Revealed in the Aaron-era *Original Sin*: a daughter of Odin and Freyja, taken by the Angels of the Tenth Realm and raised in Heven, ignorant of her Asgardian origins for most of her life. Her existence was concealed from the subject for his entire prior life, by his parents, as part of the settlement that ended the Asgard–Heven war.

**Dynamic.** Cautious, mutually curious, and notably *not* trauma-bonded. The subject approaches Angela as an adult approaches an adult relative discovered late — with interest and goodwill but without the regressive pulls that Odin and Loki trigger. Angela, raised outside the Odinic system, does not activate his old reflexes; she also does not give him the unconditional acceptance Frigga did. The relationship sits in a healthier middle register than any other Asgardian-family bond in the file.

**Evolution.** From revelation to wary alliance to functional siblinghood across the Aaron and post-Aaron material. The relationship has not been tested by major betrayal and so its resilience under stress is undetermined.

**Trust / safety.** Provisional, accruing.

**Failure mode.** The concealment itself is a wound he has not fully addressed: his parents lied to him about her existence for centuries. That this has not produced the explosive paternal confrontation one might expect is, again, ambiguous — possibly integration, possibly avoidance.

**Leverage.** Low-to-moderate. Angela is harder to weaponize against him than Loki or Balder because the bond is younger and her own agency is formidable. Most useful as corroborating evidence in any campaign aimed at the broader Odin-deception theme.

---

### 6.7 TYR — Half-Brother (Paternal)

**History.** Established Asgardian figure across decades; sibling-rivalry dynamic foregrounded particularly in the Aaron run.

**Dynamic.** The subject has, in Tyr, the brother whose grievance most resembles Loki's — the second son, less favored, watching the golden elder collect what feels like undeserved love — but expressed through soldierly resentment rather than sorcerous treachery. The subject's behavior with Tyr is correspondingly more guarded than with Balder and more workable than with Loki: he registers the resentment, declines to be governed by it, and extends fraternal respect without expecting fraternal warmth in return.

**Trust / safety.** Functional but cool.

**Failure mode.** Tyr will not have the subject's back at the margin; the subject knows this and does not pretend otherwise. The relationship's stability comes from accurate mutual appraisal rather than from affection.

**Leverage.** Modest. Tyr could be recruited as a domestic political opponent without much difficulty; the subject would not be surprised and would not be destabilized.

---

### 6.8 MAGNI AND MODI — Sons (Across Possible Futures and Emerging in Current Canon)

**History.** The mythological sons of Thor, instantiated across various Marvel timelines — most prominently in the *Earth X* material and Aaron's King Thor far-future arc, with current-canon appearances developing in late material.

**Dynamic.** What is documented is paternal in a register the subject has had to construct from negative example. He knows what Odin did wrong and is visibly determined not to reproduce it: he praises both feats and mercies, refuses to set the sons in competition, refrains from the identity-conditional language that Odin used on him. In the King Thor arc, his old age is softened by the relationship with these descendants in a way that contrasts sharply with how Odin entered old age (imprisoned, raging, alone).

**Evolution.** From speculative-future cameo to integrated late-canon presence. The relationship is, by design and across writers, the *correction* of the Odin pattern — the subject's most visible piece of generational repair work.

**Trust / safety.** High; the subject extends to them the unconditional acceptance he did not receive.

**Failure mode.** Overcorrection. The risk in the documented material is that he will be insufficiently demanding where Odin was excessively so — but this is a small fault compared to its predecessor.

**Leverage.** High through threat (any child in danger activates the maximum protective response), low through manipulation (the sons are too young and too central to be turned).

---

### 6.9 THE DAUGHTERS OF THUNDER (Atli, Frigg, Ellisiv) — Granddaughters (King Thor Arc)

**History.** Aaron's far-future King Thor material. The granddaughters reign with him at the end of all things and represent the subject's late-life relational achievement: a household organized around mutual love rather than conditional judgment.

**Dynamic.** The grandfatherly register is the warmest the subject ever achieves in the record. With these descendants he is teasing, indulgent, proud without need, and entirely free of the worthiness anxiety that has organized his life. They love him without testing him; he loves them without conditioning the love. It is, structurally, what he never had with Odin and what he has spent millennia trying to construct.

**Trust / safety.** Total, within the future-tense frame in which they exist.

**Failure mode.** None internal. The arc is elegiac and any threat to the granddaughters is the engine of his final battles.

**Leverage.** Maximum through threat; the subject will, demonstrably, give his life. None through any other vector.

---

### 6.10 THE DEAD — Cumulative Bereavement Load

A complete family-and-origin map must register the dead as a category, because the subject's bereavement load is one of the largest in the available record and operates as an ambient psychological pressure independent of any single relationship.

**The accumulated list, by approximate chronology of impact:** Donald Blake (in the sense that the Blake identity has been retired and mourned as a self); Odin (multiple deaths, most consequentially in the Surtur war and again in late Aaron); Frigga (death in Aaron's Realm War); Balder (recurrently); the half-brother whose death triggered the *Blood and Thunder* madness; Jane Foster (twice, once as Thor); innumerable comrades from the Warriors Three and the long Asgardian roster; the Asgardians lost in Ragnarok and in the Siege of Asgard; the alternate-future losses he has been shown and has had to carry forward; the cosmic-scale bereavements of the Black Winter arc.

**Pattern.** The subject metabolizes individual losses with culturally appropriate ritual — toasts, funerary speech, the heroic dedication of subsequent action to the lost. He does *not* metabolize the cumulative load. Across the late runs the bereavement compounds into an ambient melancholy that the kingship intensifies because the throne is the position from which all the dead are most visible. The Reigning failure mode is, in this light, less a discrete decompensation than the predictable end-state of un-metabolized cumulative grief plus power.

**Leverage.** Any actor capable of forcing the subject to *count* his dead in compressed time — a list, a ritual, a confrontation with their


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Sub-file to the Odinson dossier. The subject's relational footprint is wide and deep; this section maps the people he chose, in distinction to those he was born to (Section I). The methodology is constant across entries: provenance and first-contact circumstance, longitudinal dynamic, calibrated trust-and-safety reading, the specific failure mode of the dyad, and the leverage an informed adversary (or an informed friend) could apply through it.*

*Caveat. The subject is, by relational temperament, a fast-bonder and a slow-releaser. He will move someone from stranger to brother in a single shared engagement and will keep them on that ledger for centuries. This makes the leverage map below larger than it would be for a comparably aged human, and the protective imperative through any of these vectors correspondingly stronger.*

---

## 1. SIF — sword-sister, longest-standing peer attachment

**Provenance.** Asgardian, raised in proximity from childhood; sparring partner from before either could lift their own weapon. The relationship predates language for both parties in any meaningful sense and is the only one in the file with no discoverable "first meeting" — they are each other's furniture.

**Longitudinal dynamic.** Three discernible eras. (a) *Childhood-through-Lee/Kirby:* fraternal-coded, with Sif as one of the boys; her later transformation into the warrior-woman of the Vanaheim cycles is the formal beginning of the romantic register. (b) *Conway/Wein/Thomas through Simonson:* primary on-shelf partner, with Jane Foster orbiting in the background; the relationship is competent, adult, and characterized by mutual professional respect. The subject defers to her tactical judgment in field, which he does for almost no one else. (c) *Post-Disassembled through Aaron/Cates:* relationship sublimated into deep comradeship; periodic re-ignitions; she is the person he most readily seeks out when he is hurt and does not want a witness who will fuss.

**Trust and safety.** Maximal. She is one of perhaps three people permitted to see him cry without his managing his face for them, and the only person to whom he will admit physical pain in the moment of feeling it rather than after. He sleeps in her presence — a clinically significant marker; his REM tells (described elsewhere in the file: jaw working, the left hand curling as if to grip a haft) are observable only by people he does not guard against.

**Failure mode.** The relationship fails on the same axis it succeeds: she is too embedded to be heard when she disagrees with him on first-order matters. During the Reigning trajectory, hers is among the voices that should have stopped him and did not, because he had long since folded her into a baseline of *people who love me and therefore will accept this.* The dyad's risk is consensual blindness, not conflict.

**Leverage.** Any threat to Sif produces the highest-amplitude protective response in his repertoire short of one directed at Jane. She is also the person whose disapproval — *delivered cold, not hot* — can stop him mid-action when no force in nine realms can. An adversary with access to her voice (illusion, coercion, simulacrum) holds a key to his decision loop; he has been exploited this way more than once and will be again because the trust is not revocable.

---

## 2. THE WARRIORS THREE

### 2a. FANDRAL

**Provenance.** Asgardian noble cohort, age-mates. First-contact era is pre-published; functionally a fixture from childhood.

**Dynamic.** Fandral is the friend who allows the subject to be *light*. In Fandral's company the subject's laugh-pattern (mapped in Section VII: a two-stage release — short bark, then sustained roll — that is rare outside specific company) is most reliably elicited. Together they perform an old comedic routine of mock-rivalry over women, drink, and feats, which is functionally a regulation strategy: the subject uses Fandral to reset his nervous system after heavy engagements. There is an unspoken understanding that Fandral does not bring up Odin, Loki, or worthiness; the friendship has a maintained perimeter and both parties keep it.

**Trust and safety.** High but specifically calibrated: trusted absolutely in the field and in revelry, less so in serious counsel. The subject does not bring grief to Fandral; he brings appetite.

**Failure mode.** When Fandral has been hurt or killed (the Aaron-era casualties of the Realm War; previous mortalities undone by reincarnation) the subject's grief is disproportionate to the conscious affection because the loss is of a *regulation channel*, not merely a person. He loses access to his own lightness.

**Leverage.** Threats to Fandral produce reliable rescue behavior of an impulsive, plan-poor kind — the subject will charge without staging because the affect is pre-rational.

### 2b. HOGUN

**Provenance.** Foreign-born (Vanir-coded across most renderings), brought into the warrior cohort by elective bond rather than birth. This matters: Hogun is the subject's first *chosen* brother and the proof-of-concept for the later expatriate pattern.

**Dynamic.** Inverse-amplitude friendship. Hogun speaks rarely; the subject talks more in Hogun's presence than in any other, as if filling the silence is part of the contract. The friendship runs on shared silences in field and the subject's monologues over fires. Hogun's gravity functions as a counterweight; the subject has been observed to physically slow his pace when walking beside him, matching cadence.

**Trust and safety.** High in a different register from Fandral: trusted in counsel precisely because Hogun will not flatter. When the subject wants to know whether an act is honorable he asks Hogun and accepts whatever monosyllable returns.

**Failure mode.** Hogun's reticence allows the subject to project consent onto silence. He has at least twice mistaken Hogun's withheld objection for Hogun's agreement and proceeded.

**Leverage.** Hogun's word, weaponized (forgery, illusion, possession), would carry weight against the subject's better instincts because the subject treats Hogun as a moral instrument.

### 2c. VOLSTAGG

**Provenance.** Asgardian, family-man, elder of the cohort.

**Dynamic.** Volstagg is the subject's exposure to *domesticity*. Volstagg's hall, full of children and disorder, is one of the few rooms in any realm where the subject's shoulders drop on entry — a postural tell observable across multiple writers. The subject brings Volstagg's children small carved gifts (referenced as a habit in the Simonson and Aaron eras), and remembers their names and ages without rehearsal — a memory load he does not extend to political acquaintances. Volstagg's grief at the loss of his own children during the *Siege* fallout was, for the subject, one of the heaviest losses of his life *by proxy* — he has spoken of it in Aaron's run as a wound he carries on Volstagg's behalf.

**Trust and safety.** Total in the personal register; tactically, the subject does not always defer to Volstagg's judgment but always to Volstagg's *heart*.

**Failure mode.** The subject infantilizes Volstagg's pain — wants to fix it, cannot, withdraws from him during periods when Volstagg is most bereaved because the impossibility of repair is intolerable. This is one of the only documented patterns of the subject *avoiding* a loved one in grief.

**Leverage.** Threats to Volstagg's family are among the most reliable triggers for warrior's madness onset in the file.

---

## 3. BETA RAY BILL

**Provenance.** *Thor* #337 (Simonson). An alien horse-faced champion bound to a dying people, who in the most diagnostically important encounter of the subject's middle life lifted Mjolnir.

**Dynamic.** This is the relationship that *should not have worked* on the subject's stated psychology and does, which is one of the strongest pieces of evidence for the dossier's overall conclusion that he is not narcissistically organized. A stranger lifts his hammer — the externalized arbiter of his worth — and within hours the subject has fought him to a draw, brokered a compromise with Odin to commission Stormbreaker, and accepted Bill as a peer-brother. There is no recorded sulking, no diminishment, no period of cooled distance. He bonds *because* Bill was worthy, not in spite of it.

**Trust and safety.** Maximal-with-distance. Bill operates in deep space; contact is intermittent; when it occurs the bond resumes mid-sentence. The subject treats Bill as a true equal in a way he treats almost no one else — no big-brother register, no protective overlay. This is the relationship that *flatters his psychology least* and is therefore among the healthiest in the file.

**Failure mode.** Distance allows idealization. The subject has never had to live with Bill across the duration of an actual disagreement that mattered; the relationship is untested by proximity.

**Leverage.** A call from Bill receives priority routing. Bill's endorsement of an act would shift the subject's behavior more than most counselors could. Because the friendship is honor-grounded rather than affection-grounded, a credible appeal to Bill's *judgment* of him is potent.

---

## 4. JANE FOSTER — see Section I (romantic), partial cross-reference here

Jane appears under Romantic, but her *chosen-family* register requires a note: in the Aaron era when she became Thor, the subject's response was an absence — of resentment, of competitive feeling, of the predicted narcissistic injury. He carried her hammer when she could not, he stood at her side as second, he sat with her through chemotherapy in human guise. The dyad shifted from romantic-active to something closer to twinned vocation. When she died (twice, per canon's accounting) the subject's grief operated at the structural level of the file's worst losses. The leverage here is total and is mapped in Section I.

---

## 5. THE AVENGERS — chosen polity

The Avengers are not one relationship but a *category*: the chosen mortal-and-allied fellowship that has, since *Avengers* #1, supplied the subject's primary stable peer environment. The general pattern is collegial-with-warmth; the specific dyads vary.

### 5a. STEVE ROGERS

**Provenance.** *Avengers* #4 onward. Two figures with congruent moral grammars — both honor-coded, both protective by orientation, both quietly suspicious of cleverness.

**Dynamic.** The subject defers tactically to Rogers in a way he defers to almost no other mortal, and accepts Rogers's leadership without it costing him pride. The two have a documented habit, across decades of issues, of brief private conferences before engagements — a quick exchange of nods or murmured words at the staging point — that function as mutual ground-checking. The subject seeks out Rogers after losses; Rogers seeks out the subject after morally ambiguous orders. The relationship is reciprocally regulating.

**Trust and safety.** Among the highest in his Earth file. He has, on record, accepted Rogers's correction in front of others without face-saving theater — a capacity he extends to few.

**Failure mode.** Both men are pridefully honorable; when they disagree on a matter of honor they cannot easily bridge it (the various Civil War-era rifts). The dyad fails not on weak bonds but on incompatible moral algorithms applied with equal force.

**Leverage.** Rogers can ask things of the subject that no one else can. A counterfeit Rogers — and there have been several — receives unwarranted credit before the subject's pattern-recognition catches up.

### 5b. TONY STARK

**Provenance.** Founding cohort. The dyad is structurally tense — a god of intuition partnered with a man of stratagem — and that tension is itself the bond.

**Dynamic.** Affectionate antagonism with intermittent peaks of deep loyalty. The subject does not understand Stark's technology and does not pretend to; Stark does not understand the subject's metaphysics and pretends to. Both forgive the other's ignorance because each genuinely respects the other's competence in the unshared domain. The subject drinks with Stark in a different register than with anyone else — slower, more conversational, with Stark on water and the subject pacing himself in honor of that — a quiet act of solidarity around Stark's sobriety arcs that he has never spoken of aloud, which is the most Stark-coded gesture in his repertoire.

**Trust and safety.** Moderate-to-high, asymmetric. The subject trusts Stark with his life and not always with his judgment. The Civil War rupture wounded him in a way he has not fully discussed in any canonical interior monologue.

**Failure mode.** Stark's cleverness activates the subject's Loki-allergy. When Stark moves opaquely, the subject's prior pattern leaks into the present and he overreacts. Stark has learned to telegraph more than he otherwise would.

**Leverage.** Stark's *manipulated* counsel is dangerous — the subject treats Stark's advice as an object to be skeptically weighed but acted upon when the weighing returns balanced, and a corrupted Stark vector has been used against him.

### 5c. BRUCE BANNER / HULK

**Provenance.** Early Avengers. The relationship is two-tracked because the man and the rage-state are different counterparts.

**Dynamic.** With Banner: gentle. The subject calibrates volume, presence, and physical proximity downward when speaking with Banner — visible across multiple artists — and asks after his welfare with a care he reserves for the constitutionally fragile. With the Hulk: a roughhousing fraternal affection of physical competition that the subject genuinely enjoys; the Hulk is one of the few beings on Earth he can hit at full strength without restraint, and the *somatic relief* of unguarded contact is part of the bond. Their fights are courtship.

**Trust and safety.** With Banner: high in personal register. With the Hulk: trusted absolutely in combat, distrusted in social environments where collateral matters.

**Failure mode.** The subject confuses the cathartic value of fighting the Hulk with the Hulk's wellbeing; he can underweight the cost to Banner.

**Leverage.** Threats to Banner produce a quiet, focused protective response unlike his usual register.

### 5d. CLINT BARTON

**Dynamic.** Affectionate ribbing. Barton calls the subject by diminutives no one else uses ("big guy," "point break" in some renderings); the subject permits it as he permits little else, which is its own diagnostic. The subject's tolerance for mortal cheek is wide, but his *enjoyment* of it is narrower, and Barton sits inside the narrower band.

**Trust and safety.** High, casual, unceremonious — the register the subject most badly needs and has the hardest time generating from his own side.

**Failure mode.** The subject's protectiveness reads, to Barton, as condescension; minor abrasion, periodic flares.

### 5e. NATASHA ROMANOFF

**Dynamic.** Cool mutual respect of two operators who recognize each other's competence and decline to be sentimental about it. The subject does not perform charm for Romanoff and Romanoff does not perform anything for him. The encounters are economical.

**Trust and safety.** High-and-bounded. He trusts her judgment; he does not always trust the *framing* of the information she chooses to share.

**Failure mode.** Romanoff's death affected him at the centuries-scale even though their bond was the most reserved of the Avenger dyads — diagnostic of how he ledgers loyalty over warmth.

### 5f. CAROL DANVERS

**Dynamic.** Late-period sibling-coded peer with comparable power-class. The two have a documented pattern of competitive drinking that they both treat as serious sport. The subject treats Danvers as one of perhaps five mortals (counting Captain Marvel as functionally so) whose physical authority he never reflexively manages around.

**Trust and safety.** High; she is one of the few people who can tell him he is wrong and have him reconsider without an intervening period of sulking.

**Failure mode.** Their similarities — pride, frontal engagement, intolerance for being managed — multiply rather than complement when stressed.

---

## 6. ERIC MASTERSON — protégé and replacement

**Provenance.** DeFalco/Frenz era. A mortal architect bonded to the subject's identity by Odin's decree after a near-fatal injury; later separated and given the mace Thunderstrike to operate as his own hero.

**Dynamic.** The first of the subject's protégés and the one he was least prepared to have. The relationship begins as forced cohabitation of identity (echoing the Blake configuration in inverse) and matures into mentorship. The subject's willingness to *equip a successor* — to grant the mace, to endorse the independent operation — is a sign of his unusual ego-permeability around the role.

**Trust and safety.** High; deeply mourned at death.

**Failure mode.** The subject has tended, ever since Masterson, to mentor mortals with a slight overshadow — to remain in the room even when his absence is what would serve them. Masterson's death calcified this protectiveness.

**Leverage.** Masterson's son (Kevin) remains in the file as a long-tail responsibility the subject has quietly honored across decades.

---

## 7. THROG / SIMON WALTERSON — the frog cohort

A note often dismissed as comic relief. During the Simonson frog arc, the subject (transformed) led a frog uprising in Central Park and afterward did not forget the frogs. Throg has been a recurring minor ally; the subject treats him as a legitimate peer warrior and refers to him without irony. This is small-stakes evidence of the subject's bedrock trait: *honor extended downward through scale* without condescension. He owes the frogs and pays.

---

## 8. ROZ SOLOMON — terrestrial liaison

**Provenance.** Aaron run. A SHIELD agent assigned environmental crimes; brought into the subject's life during the post-Whisper period.

**Dynamic.** Brief romantic resonance subordinated to working partnership. Solomon is the only person in the Unworthy period who could request the subject's presence and receive it without pretext. She represented to him an unsentimental *competence about Earth* that he needed when he was least himself.

**Trust and safety.** High in field, bounded in personal. The relationship cooled rather than ruptured.

**Leverage.** Solomon's professional jeopardy receives priority response.

---

## 9. SCREWBEARD, the Dwarven smiths of Nidavellir

**Provenance.** Recurring across runs; primary craftsmen of his hammers and prostheses.

**Dynamic.** The subject's relationship with the smiths is the closest he comes to a *guild* membership outside Asgard. He defers to their craft entirely, observes their protocols, and pays in feats and protection rather than coin. His uru arm was forged by them; his trust of the prosthetic is partially a trust of its makers.

**Failure mode.** When the smiths are coerced (Malekith era), the subject's response is disproportionate — he treats the violation of the forge as a violation of a sanctum.

---

## 10. SKURGE THE EXECUTIONER — posthumous bond

**Provenance.** Initially a Loki-aligned antagonist; redeemed in Simonson's *Thor* #362 at Gjallerbru, holding the bridge against the legions of Hel so the subject and others could escape.

**Dynamic.** The most important *posthumous* relationship in the file. The subject did not befriend Skurge in life; he honors him in death. References to Gjallerbru recur across the subject's interior monologues for thirty-five published years; the standard he holds himself to in last-stand moments is *Skurge's* standard. The Executioner became, in death, one of the subject's internalized voices.
